0393338959|5.0|Joseph Stiglitz, the 2001 Nobel prize winning Economist, makes a very powerful case of how the Wall Street took the main street for a ride while the regulators turned a blind eye while the housing bubble was growing. While the avarice of the few held the whole country, and eventually the whole world to a ransom. The unrepentant perpetuators were eventually bailed out by the government, the same government which is an anathema to their business while in good times. The ungrateful then brushed off the whole disaster as a statistical event which no one could have foreseen, and patted themselves on their back and rewarded themselves lavishly. And back to business as usual. In the following paragraphs I have selected a few key thoughts which I found to be very profound and very insightful.    Markets lie at the heart of every successful economy, but markets do not work well on their own. It is in tradition of Keynes. America's financial markets failed to perform their essential societal functions of managing risk, allocating capital, and mobilizing savings while keeping transaction costs low. Instead they created risk, misallocated capital, and encouraged excessive indebtedness while imposing high transaction costs.     The reason why the Banks are regulated is that their failure can cause harm to rest of the economy. A country in which socialism is often treated as an anathema, has socialized risk and intervened in the markets in unprecedented ways.     Securitization of mortgages created asymmetries. The buyer of the security typically knew less about the borrowers than the Bank or the firm that had originated the mortgage. Because the originator did not bear the consequences of the mistakes, the incentives for doing a good job at credit assessment were greatly attenuated. Globalization had opened up a whole world of fools; many investors abroad did not understand America's peculiar mortgage market, especially the idea of non recourse mortgages (where the borrower canjust turn his or her keys over to the creditor with no further consequences). Originators gave away lot of money to many unworthy borrowers, throwing caution to the winds, and sold the rotten apples to the Investment Bankers, who were lot more sophisticated than gullible. The mortgage originators made their money in form of huge fees (what Stiglitz calls as high transaction costs).    Investment Bankers just peddled the rotten apples, with the collusion of the rating agencies, to investors all over the world. The rating agencies did badly because they used the same bad models that the investment bankers used. They assumed that the housing prices will never decline. Another assumption was that the foreclosures in different parts of the country are not correlated. In the greed that gripped the nation, there were no holds barred-including exploiting the weakest in our society. Wall Street had used its power & money to buy deregulation. Too-big-to-fail, too-big-to-resolve, or too-big-to-manage banks are too-big-to-ignore campaign contributors.      This contrasts with the traditional situations where Banks with a long-standing relation with the community had an incentive to treat borrowers who got into trouble well; if there was a good chance that borrowers would catch up on their payments if they were given some time, then the bank would give them the time they needed. But the distant holders of the mortgages had no interest in the community and no concern about having a reputation as a good lender. Securitization made re-negotiating of mortgages difficult.     Stiglitz argues that the best option for the country is to lower the principal, not just stretching out the repayment timeline for extended fees. No one gains from forcing homeowners from their homes. Foreclosures lower the prices of other homes in the neighborhood. Thereby increasing the chances of those other homes going into foreclosure. In 2005 President Bush passed a law making it difficult for homeowners to declare bankruptcy. Banks have since then opposed repealing this law.     Stiglitz refutes the argument that lower taxes and deregulation is the mantra. He gives the example of Sweden which has one of the highest per capita income and higher life spans, but has higher tax rates. On the contrary, the financial debacle was a private sector led mis-allocation of resources on a massive scale, aided by deregulation. He states that Capitalism cannot work if private rewards are unrelated to social returns.    Stiglitz contrasts the solutions used by US during the crisis with whatever the western countries were lecturing the rest of the world a decade earlier during the Asian crisis. To pull US out of the hole, the country engaged in massive increases in spending and massive deficits, even as interest rates were brought down to zero. Banks were bailed out. These are counter cyclical monetary & fiscal policies. But developing countries were forced to follow pro-cyclical policies a decade back, i.e. cutting expenditures, raising taxes and interest rates.     Stiglitz refuses to believe the story that the Fed could not do anything to prevent the cirsis. Fed underestimated the extent of correlation between different housing markets, and overestimated the diversification of risk. It underestimated the incentives for excessive risk taking and overestimated the competence of the bankers at risk management. He states that the Fed could have raised margin requirements. In 1994, Congress gave Fed the powers to regulate the mortgage market, but Greenspan refused to use it. Fed could have reduced the loan to value ratios as the likelihood of a bubble increased. It could have lowered the maximum house payment to income ratios. It could have restricted variable rate mortgages. It could have restricted negative amortization and low-documentation loans.     He concludes by giving an example of how a failed CEO would react in different cultures. The failed CEO would commit harikari (suicide) in Japan. In UK, the CEO would resign. In US, the CEO would stay on and fight shamelessly for more bonus.    The Nobel Prize winning Economist, very strongly, more than adequately, and eloquently, made his point.

They have a soft spot for the housemaid Evangeline who eventually becomes their father's love interest.<br /><br />At first, their pranks make them endearing, as when they fool their latest Nanny into thinking they've eaten their baby sister, but the film doesn't progress very far before they become simply and unbearably rude and obnoxious in a way and to a degree that middle class English children of the Victorian Age would never have been. Fifty years after MARY POPPINS we've apparently forgotten what the values of that era were.<br /><br />Enter Nanny McPhee (Thompson), a frightening wart-nosed, snaggle-toothed magic Nanny, essentially Mary Poppins' \\"black swan.\\" Where Poppins is stern, McPhee is mean; where Poppins is mysterious, McPhee is scary; where Poppins is kindly yet firm, McPhee is commanding and domineering. The children listen to Mary Poppins because they come to respect and to love her. The children listen to McPhee because they come to respect and to fear her.<br /><br />Even though Nanny McPhee's ugliness recedes as the children's behavior improves, the film, in adopting a pragmatic approach to the use of lies, has odd ethics and a curious lack of heart. That lack of heart guarantees that NANNY MCPHEE will never become a classic.<br /><br />MARY POPPINS was the first movie my mother ever took me to see back in 1964, and I happily remembered so much of it. NANNY MCPHEE would have had me cowering under my seat, scared to go to sleep at night. Far better to turn to Julie Andrews' memorable and musical performances than to this frenetic and vicious reimagining. Yet again, in our time cruelty seems to substitute for comedy and humiliation for entertainment.night. 0470876417|4.0|This book is getting buzz in the startup community these days.  I read through it a week or so ago, and it is a useful addition to the entrepreneur's library.    The book has five major sections.  In the first, the authors introduce their notion of a business model canvas broken down into nine constituent parts.  Some of these parts are obvious things that anyone trying to think about a business model would examine such as customer segments, value proposition, channels, customer relationships and revenue streams.  The canvas adds in the other pieces (key resources, key activities, key partnerships and cost structure) and adds an organizational overlay that lends itself to analysis.  The second major section takes a look at five different business models and fits them into the canvas.  The third section, called \\"Design,\\" describes various techniques such as visual thinking, ideation and prototyping, \\"that can help you design better and more innovative business models.\\"  The Strategy section then takes the earlier concepts and combines them with other strategic approaches such as SWOT analysis and Blue Ocean strategies in order to determine where opportunities for improving models may lie.  In the final major section, \\"Process,\\" the authors lay out a roadmap for running a business model design initiative.    Note that much of the book relies heavily on concepts and work originated by others.  This is actually a great strength since it pulls together a lot of different theories and presents them in an easily digested form.  The writing is accessible, and it may be that many of the concepts are more easily absorbed here than in the original source materials (all of which are conveniently referenced in case you want to explore further).      The notion of the canvas is new, and it does have an organizational elegance about it that is appealing.  One of the points in the first section is that the first five constituent parts of a business model are all about value, whereas the final four are all about efficiency.  This makes it easy to see why it made sense for Amazon to unbundle its businesses into an e-commerce line and an IT infrastructure management line.    Aside from a few styling miscues (tiny white print on a black background on some of the pages, really), the major complaint about the book from me -- and many others -- is that it lacks depth.  Most of the case studies are short and the answers are dished up without consideration of the iterations that must have been required to get there.  This book is trying to present a generalized way of looking at business models -- and I would say that it succeeds very well at that task -- but nothing beats seeing the concepts applied in real time rather than in retrospect.  For people that are interested in seeing the canvas in action, I highly recommend reading about the Lean Launchpad project at Stanford on Steve Blank's blog.  Think about it as an advanced class after you read the book in the introductory class.

B003Y5H5KQ|4.0|\\"Hanna\\" is a ruthless, relentless, chase featuring a teen girl assassin. It's violent and suspenseful, fast and cruel. The difference is, of course, that the hero is not a muscle-bound adult male, but a barely muscled teenage girl, Saorise Ronan. And the focus never waivers from the chase. Hanna gets no love interest.<br /><br />I generally don't go to movies I know are going to be violent and I have walked out of violent films, but I loved \\"Hanna\\" and kept my eyes open throughout almost all of it. It's not just that Hanna, the assassin, is a girl, and I'm female, too, although that was part of it. \\"Hanna\\" depicts Hanna as having an admirable work ethic, and as being an underdog. The one thing that separates her from the people she so determinedly wounds, beats, stabs, and kills is that she is just following the pedagogical training of her admirably dedicated, old-school patriarch, Erik (Eric Bana.) Erik raised his girl to know how to read, write, and kill. He did this for a righteous reason. The people Erik trained Hanna to kill are very, very bad people, and the audience wants them dead, as well. The morality and worldview of \\"Hanna\\" is very much that of the setting of its opening scene: the harsh north. This is Nordic morality, where even if you do the right thing, you may end up dead, anyway. There is no reward. If you don't end up dead, and live on, you live on only to keep fighting in a harsh, unforgiving landscape.<br /><br />Saorise Ronan gives an Oscar-worthy performance as Hanna. She truly inhabits a character whose sole focus is on struggle. She enters every new landscape, from a treeless desert to a hotel room, with the focus of a wild animal: where is the food? She has a killer's eyes throughout the entire film. I have to wonder if this wasn't a tough film for her to make. Playing Hanna would have given me nightmares.<br /><br />The film's ruthlessness becomes a bit of a failing. I was no longer surprised when a character showed some humanity only to be dispatched in the most hateful of ways. 0142437611|3.0|A day in the life of a loser.<br />Considering that the day holds the life history of the last 25 years, this 1955 novella is rather action packed. Bellow obviously needed a breath of fresh air after the endless (and to me disappointing) Augie March. So he went for the often satisfactory novella form rather than the shapelessness of his fat novel.<br /><br />The loser is Wilky Adler, a fair-haired hippopotamus in his own words, now in his mid 40s and a total failure. He even lost his name: during a failed attempt at a Hollywood career he changed it to become Tommy Wilhelm. By that time he had already become a failed son: a college drop-out, the first in a family of academic distinction. On the day of the action, Tommy lives in the same hotel in Manhattan as his nearly 80 y old father, a retired medical doctor and widower. TW has moved out from his wife and two kids, because of a younger woman, and he has also lost his job as a sales executive. He hardly knows how to pay for family support. His girl friend insists that he gets a divorce before continuing their relationship. He has given his last few hundred dollars to a con man for market speculation. His well-to-do father refuses to help him out with money.<br />As the song says: if it wasn't for bad luck, he wouldn't have no luck at all.<br /><br />The narration is appealing in the sense that the dreariness has an aspect of hilarious humor. We follow TW from his hotel room down to the lobby, where he buys a newspaper and chats with the kiosk operator, to the breakfast room, where he meets with his father and another hotel guest. Then he meets his `business partner', the man who dragged him into `investing in lard'. The man claims to be a psychotherapist, and he talks freely about the most colorful case histories. It seems quite obvious that he is an impostor, but his stories are tasty. He is the one who advises TW to' seize the day!' With hindsight, that can only be seen as blackest humor.<br /><br />We learn all about TW's life and habits from his own thoughts and recollections as well as from the dialogues and from the other people's silent observations on him.<br />Bellow is the all-knowing third person narrator, who assembles inner monologues from different people. We learn through their eyes that Tommy is untidy, even dirty. He is notoriously unable to follow his better instincts. He will decide not to do something and then he does it anyway. He needs uppers to get through his days. A mess of a man. He is to blame for everything bad that happened to him, because of his inability to be responsible.<br />It ends in a total dead-end: his investment is wiped out. He has nothing left. He needs to sell his junk heap of a car to pay his hotel bill. His day ends at a funeral of a stranger, where he sheds bitter tears for himself.<br /><br />Tommy was played by Robin Williams in a 2003 film, which I have not watched, and I don't quite know why I would want to. There is nothing appealing about the man. The novella needed to be as short as it is, because otherwise nobody could stay with it long enough. Is it a masterpiece as most reviewers seem to say? I am not sure. After finishing it, I wonder if the humor that is in the dialogues and the observations about the man is not of a toxic kind.his own thoughts and recollections as well as from the dialogues and from the other people's silent observations on him. B004FN7ZOC|4.0|Amon Amarth has been one of the most consistent bands of the last decade, releasing quality album after quality album, staying true to their fans, and staying true to their style.  This album continues that successful trend; that much, is for sure.  This is definitely still Amon Amarth.  I do have problems with this album though.  From a song writing standpoint, it is a good album, but structurally the songs are safer and more formula than previous efforts.  This album is probably their lightest album in their storied collection and I can't help but get a feeling that Amon Amarth is trying to appeal to more mainstream metal fans and increase their sales and fan-base.  The trademark tremolo rhythm section that Amon Amarth has become known for is significantly diminished on this album, making the overall sound lighter and more melodic and friendly to mainstream metal fans.  The riffs themselves are not as dark and a little easier to digest than previous works, not that Amon Amarth was ever really an extremely heavy band.  Really, this album is a thrash album with death growl vocals.  Which is fine, but being a death metal fan and a fan of this band, I am not as happy with this direction.<br /><br />Overall, this is a good listen, but not nearly as good as their past 3 albums that preceded it.  I would recommend this to any of the lighter metal fans that need a \\"gateway\\" album to expand their minds into heavier metal and I would recommend this to Amon Amarth faithfuls as it is still a good album.  Fans of harder death metal such as Deicide or Morbid Angel (or even harder such as Origin or Nile), may think this album is pretty weak.  I personally think it is a little weak for this band and hope that their next release goes in a more sinister direction than this formula work.

B0034G4P7G|4.0|THE SOCIAL NETWORK is nonlinear but still always makes sense, which is trickier than it looks. I'm not sure if it's really \\"the best movie of the year\\" -- it's actually a small story, for all of the billions Zuckerberg was eventually worth, since it's just about how one guy had an idea and pursued it even when that meant screwing over all of his friends and acquaintances -- but it's certainly a good movie, which is definitely something.<br /><br />I should back up slightly, for coherency's sake: THE SOCIAL NETWORK is the story of Mark Zuckerberg, who founded the social networking Internet site Facebook.com, and is currently worth north of a dozen billion dollars. In the time-honored way of tech pioneers, Zuckerberg founded Facebook while he was still an undergraduate (at Harvard -- the other time-honored part of the equation is that the pioneer must be a privileged over-achiever to begin with) about seven years ago, and the movie runs through roughly the first year of Facebook, from the night Zuckerberg threw up a \\"rate the girls of Harvard\\" site called Facemash.com to get back at the girlfriend who just dumped him to the point where he'd driven out, locked out, outmaneuvered, or alienated all of the people who had helped get Facebook started.<br /><br />Of course, that's this movie's angle; presumably Zuckerberg himself would have a different story. SOCIAL NETWORK is based very heavily on the depositions from two suits brought against Zuckerberg: one from his former best friend and the original \\"outside man\\" for Facebook, and one from a pair of vastly more overprivileged twin rowers, who seem like a gift from heaven to make Zuckerberg seem more likable in comparison with their endless smug rich-prep pose of superiority. SOCIAL NETWORK keeps looping back to those two deposition sessions, presumably because the events of them are legally guaranteed, but that keeps framing this as a story about money, when it should be a movie about connection. It is, more or less, but it could have been more visually inventive -- it's an old-fashioned, plodding sort of movie to look at, for all its zippy modern subject matter -- and could have either focused more tightly on what Facebook was -- why it was so successful, so quickly -- or on Zuckerberg himself. As it is, SOCIAL NETWORK has a bundle of good performances circling the enigma of Mark Zuckerberg, and it raises a whole lot of questions and thoughts that it isn't sure how to address.been more visually inventive -- it's an old-fashioned, plodding sort of movie to look at, for all its zippy modern subject matter -- and could have either focused more tightly on what Facebook was -- why it was so successful, so quickly -- or on Zuckerberg himself. B0026RCAGC|5.0|Having for years been perfectly satisfied with the famous Klemperer EMI studio recording and not feeling especially tempted to try the mono Testament set (also with Klemperer at the helm) recently released, I was particularly pleased to discover how satisfying this performance is. It's not perfect; Sciutti is a little tremulous in her aria and the great Birgit sometimes sounds a bit detached -and occasionally a little under the note in the middle section of her voice - but she really delivers in her big moments and Sciutti perks up both to blend and to contrast nicely with Nilsson in the ensembles. Nilsson also scales her voice down attractively when the emotion demands it; it's not all sand-blasting by any means. Bohme's rotund bass is almost as good as Frick's and he also has a lovely speaking voice - I can never understand it when actors are used for the dialogue; their voices almost never match the singers'. That's not the case here, as the singers speak their own dialogue and very well too. McCracken is really very affecting as Florestan; he has all the notes and sounds utterly distraught - close to madness as the result of his dreadful plight, heroic and unhinged. Krause is a little jolly-sounding as Pizarro but vocalises very attractively; minor parts are well taken by some fairly major voices. Maazel's direction isn't very subtle but it's certainly dramatic and both the orchestra and the chorus are terrific. I think that opera buffs like me who have been listening for years to - and sometimes through - ropey old mono recordings full of hiss and distortion need to remember that for many newer collectors the quality of sound is very important when one is getting to know a central work like \\"Fidelio\\", and certainly this 1964 set will not disappoint in its clarity, space and dynamics; it has been beautifully re-mastered. The artificial echo effect devised for the dungeon scene was controversial but I think it works and it is certainly consistent with the overall emphasis upon a dramatic, pacy, theatrical atmosphere. And when all is said and done, the two principals are really involved and involving. I don't say that this set is preferable to the EMI with the incomparable Ludwig and Vickers but it's a credible option.emphasis upon a dramatic, pacy, theatrical atmosphere. And when all is said and done, the two principals are really involved and involving. I don't say that this set is preferable to the EMI with the incomparable Ludwig and Vickers but it's a credible option.
B00000IL26|4.0|Like the great Sam Cooke, trumpet maestro Lee Morgan had the rare and unenviable distinction of being shot dead by a woman - in Morgan's case by his common-law wife Helen More, as he was preparing to start his set at Slugs Jazz Club in NYC in February 1972. So ended the short life of one of Jazz's most creative talents at the tragically young age of 33.<br /><br />The Sidewinder is of course a species of rattlesnake. This December 1963 recording is generally regarded as Morgan's most accessible work, and something of a minor (some might say major) jazz classic essential to any serious collection. With Joe Henderson - who worked as Morgan's sideman for many years - on sax and the capable Barry Harris on piano, backed by Bob Cranshaw on bass and Billy Higgins on drums, the quintet belts out some fine up-tempo numbers most of which have a 4/4 beat, so often appeal to people who don't normally like or listen to jazz but prefer more rhythmic danceable sounds. The interplay between Morgan and Henderson in particular is really first-rate and way beyond anything found in boogaloo or R&B music of the period: this makes `The Sidewinder' a kind of crossover jazz album with wide appeal.<br /><br />Five long-ish tracks (two of more than 10 minutes' each) are complemented on the modern remix by an alternative take of `Totem Pole', the album's second great composition after the title track. All the music was composed by the talented and prolific 25-year old Morgan, and the title track achieved great popular success when released as a single.<br /><br />Unlike Miles' minimalist, super-cool and clean sound or Trane's adventurous cerebral stream-of-consciousness playing, Morgan's trademark sound is upbeat, happy and enjoyable, and this is definitely a repeat-play album which gets better each time you hear it.

And it grew on me on a<br />2nd viewing. And I'm a fan of Aronofsky's. But I'm in the minority in<br />admiring this film, but not being able to completely embrace it.

ARR begins with Enzo at the end of his life confident that he will be a human soon - we know right away what Enzo's fate is.  But what is it about Enzo that makes him so certain about his rebirth? 0805066691|3.0|It has been 41 years since this best seller was published.  The book is more a historical romance than history.  Mr. Brown was not a trained historian, and does not claim to be.  The book lacks an introduction and preface as one expects in scholarly works.  While it is always a question whether even a professional historian can be objective, anyone who has read more contemporary historical works in this area will quickly see that Mr. Brown's approach lacks objectivity. A couple of examples: In Chapter Two of the book, \\"The Long Walk of the Navahos,\\" the author ends with the observation that the Navahos would be the least unfortunate of the western Indians. He conveniently omits that the Navahos number about 250,000, have a reservation about the size of West Virginia, have developed a self-sustaining tourism business with their arts and crafts, and have maintained their language and culture.  In Chapter Three, \\"Little Crow's War,\\" Mr. Brown fails to state that there was dissention among the Sioux about even starting a war, that cooler heads did not prevail, and that the Sioux warriors started the \\"war\\" by killing 400 to 500 unarmed settlers including men, women, and children which prompted calling in some military forces in the middle of the Civil War.  In several places Mr. Brown suggests that practice of mutilation including scalping had been introduced by Europeans; later study has shown scalping and mutilation were practiced by Indians before Columbus.  The events described by Mr. Brown are substantially accurate as far as they go, and there is no doubt that Indians west of the Mississippi suffered greatly under the tsunami of westward settlement in the 19th century.  My opinion, the book has been superceded by later scholarship.

I'm intrigued by the reviews that state that Hocus Pocus is the only really interesting thing on the album.  After two or three or a hundred listens, I often dropped the needle after Hocus Pocus, as it became the least interesting track on the album to me.  Yodeling is cool, but really?  It felt like shtick.  Moving Waves, as well as the second side track with piano and flute, became my two favorites.  I learned the piano riff and chord progression from side two (I-flat major III, flat major VII, I) and played them over and over and over and over...    I took this album to college, and for four years in a practice room as I studied classical piano, I played those chords to soothe myself and played Focus in my dorm room (in heavy rotation with Led Zeppelin and Mussourgsky) to keep myself sane.  As I moved through the next three decades, and my LP collection was slowly dismantled due to time, taste, loss of my turntable and many moves, I have continued to haul around my Focus albums.  They're within a few feet of me as I type-- unplayable, but beloved.     I assumed that these albums would be long out of print and unavailable.  I look forward to being able to plug them into my car and for the first time in my life, head out on an interstate with these Dutch boys wailing in my ears.  I'll have to remember to stay in my body this time...

In fact, they have become an essential litigation framework, with the new provisions they contain aimed at increasing efficiency and improving case management.<br /><br />Part of the estimable Blackstone's Guide Series, this new Second Edition contains the full updated Criminal Procedure Rules 2010, and the Consolidated Criminal Practice Direction.  The result is a comprehensive and up-to-date collection of relevant procedural materials.<br /><br />Amendments to the rules made since they were introduced mean they are now known as a `cradle to grave pathway' that practitioners must follow. B002YVAT72|4.0|I got this recently and finally set it up, connected it to my lap top, and got out the old Discwasher and fluid from 1978, and gave it a try on an album of Emmylou Harris' called Evangeline, which was never released on CD due to all the lawyers. I did not use the included \\"EZ\\" software because that automatically only creates mp3 files at 192 kbps quality. Instead I used Audacity to record each side as a long \\"wav\\" file, and then combined them into one long \\"wav\\" file of the entire album. I then used Audacity to trim out some noise from the beginning of each side (where the needle hits the record), and to do some fade outs at the end of each track, take out the worst \\"pops\\", and to create some real silence between the tracks. I then saved the file with the improvements. I have Nero CD burning software, which comes with a Wave Editor which has some capabilities that Audacity doesn't. I imported the wav file into the Nero Wave Editor, and used it to automatically break the large file into individual tracks. I saved them as both individual wav files and as 320 kbps mp3 files. From there, I burned a CD using the wav files. Then I used a free mp3 tag editing software (mp3tag) to add the Album, Artist, Song Title, Year, and cover art to the mp3 files. Finally I imported those files into iTunes.    All of that sounds like a lot, and it did take some time, but I think it was worth it. I now have a very good sounding album that I can listen to digitally anytime I want. I was so impressed that I right away ordered more Discwasher D4+ fluid from Amazon so I can get started on some of the other albums I have that never made it to CD.    I will caution you that in order to use Audacity correctly, it will require some experimentation and in making some changes via your computer's Control Panel > Sounds and Audio Devices settings to correctly set the source and playback source. In Audacity you will need to go into Preferences as well and make some changes. But it IS worth the effort. I wish I had bought one of these ION turntables a long time ago. This particular model also allows you to plug in via 1/8\\" mini stereo jack, any other sound source, such as a tape deck, old reel to reel, or whatever, and digitize those as well.    No, this is not some $300 or more turntable for super audiophiles, but given what you can do with it for the price I highly recommend it. Be sure to clean your records as best as possible. This will go a long way to determining how well the final files will sound.
0671015206|5.0|My husband and I read this book when it was first published thirteen years ago.  Some years earlier, we had observed, with amusement, two wealthy relatives pour over newspaper ads for the cheapest fares to Palm Desert, where they would winter.  With such wealth, we could not believe that they spent a half an hour just to save a mere twenty or thirty bucks...ten percent of their purchase.  But someone pointed out to us that such careful frugality was how they had become millionaires to begin with.  So when \\"The Millionaire Next Door\\" was published, I decided to learn about that frugality.      We already had practiced some of the habits of those who successfully accumulate wealth, but our lifestyle became more focussed after reading this book.  I can attest to the power of these practices.  When I read of baby-boomers who will have difficulty retiring (fewer than one in ten have saved over $100,000,) I know it doesn't apply to us, even though we have only the equivalent of a typical two-wage-earner income.  In fact, in my mid-fifties, I am already retired.  My husband will retire at age 62.  We have sent one child to the best college he could get into, a top-tier private university (read shockingly expensive!), and will soon send a second to a similar type of school.  Upon retirement, our monthly income will be about the same as our working income, and perhaps a bit higher.  We will need that income, because we plan to travel abroad regularly during our early retirement years.  We will also be relocating from a smallish city to a large metropolitan area, despite the higher cost of real estate.  Barring some unforseen circumstance, we will be able to afford these things, and in the event of some unforseen circumstance, we can weather that, too.    How did we do this?  The best, most cost-saving step we took was to live in a modest home, which significantly reduced our outlay for utilities, taxes, insurance, maintenance, and furnishings.  Speaking of furnishings, we bought good quality furniture that we expected to last 'forever,' which it has.  Yes, Pottery Barn is great, but most of our original furniture is still fine and we don't replace what is still functional and intact.  We use paint and a few accent items to update the look, but no wholesale overhauls, because that is extravagent.  We buy nice cars and maintain them scrupulously, but drive them until they are no longer operable. In fact, most of what we buy is good to better quality...just to the point that we felt the added cost was more than compensated for by longevity, and even personal preferences.  We rarely go out to eat, but instead, purchase nice t-bone steaks and cook them at home.  With a kid still at home, we get pizza once a week or so, but even that is generally take-n-bake, which we prefer.  I rarely, rarely buy coffee out anymore...even when I was still working, I brought it to work in an old-fashioned device they used to have called a Thermos....well, okay it was a stainless steel thermos I got from Starbucks for $25.00...extravagant, no?...but it has lasted forever!    Though we live in a water-based recreational area, we tolerated a goofy little boat until a really good deal came along for a used boat.  We chose to forgo the recreational vehicle, the four-wheelers, the snowmobiles, the annual winter vacations, and extravagant summer vacations.  We usually wait until movies are out on net-flix or pay-per-view, but go to the theater now and then, particularly for movies that are best seen on the big screen.  We make our own repairs.  If we don't know how to do it, we learn how its done.  We prepare our own taxes.  We keep the thermostat set at 68, and at night 65.  We turn out the lights when we leave a room. Though we are somewhat extravagent gift-givers, but don't over-indulge in daily living. Every so often, we re-evaluate where we make purchases, because what was the best deal yesterday is not always the best deal today.  We comparison shop, becausewe know that every spending decision, however small, has an impact on our future.  We don't pay for services, such as banking, that we can get for free elsewhere. We avoid credit cards.  We understand how compounded interest adds up, whether you owe or are saving.    When our oldest child was in high school, we heard complaints...\\"so-and-so has this and that and the other thing, and I KNOW they don't make as much as we do!\\"  We always answered, \\"You know they have a boat and an RV in the driveway of their beautiful home near the country-club, but you don't know how much money they owe for that stuff.\\"  Well, this recession has told us ALL how much \\"they\\" owe...alot...and they can no longer pay for it, because they never COULD afford it.  Around us, we see people losing jobs, fretting about the retirement they cannot afford.  We see foreclosures, up-side-down mortgages, and fire sales of goods.  People do not have any savings to weather a financial downturn, let alone retire.  Sadly, the chicken has come home to roost.      We, in contrast, have not blinked an eye.  We are fine, and will remain fine.  Despite the recession, we have grown our retirement nest egg, which has reached the point where it is increasing remarkably quickly. Most of all, I appreciate the sense of well-being and confidence that comes from knowing that though we have sacrificed, we have been able to do the things that are the most important to us...educate our kids and save for retirement.  (And we had the money for things that weren't our priorities, but which had to be done...in the past eight years we replaced the furnace and air conditioner, re-roofed and re-painted the house, replaced the septic system, replaced various non-functioning appliances, and did a modest kitchen update, all paid for with cash.)     This book doesn't address societal problems related to the distribution of wealth, nor does it purport to. Neither does this book provide a blue-print for how to become wealthy.  It isa report of the habits of wealthy people...habits that are surprisingly frugal.  It is simply a collection of data that may influence the habits of those who read it.      So, if you read this book, will you become a millionaire?  No, not necessariy.  At least one of the earlier comments questions the idea that you can become a millionaire merely by acting like one.  Well, that certainly is true...it depends on your income.  But, you certainly aren't going to become a millionaire by spending all of your money.  Reading this book and practicing its tenents will simply put you in the best financial position you can attain, given your income.  It just takes prioritization and sacrifice.  Look at it this way...do you want to work forever, and make deep, difficult sacrifices for the last two, three, or four decades of your life?  Or would you like to establish a comfortable, more modest lifestyle that enables you to retire?  One that you can comfortably maintain until the end of your life?    I strongly, strongly suggest this book to anyone who is ready to take responsibility for their own financial well-being.  If you are a bit of a skeptic, just read it...it can't hurt...you might have to give up two or three lattes to purchase the book, but that is just the first step in decision-making that will get you to your retirement goals.

Story is also a true art book, as the only real text in the book (aside from labels on the artwork) is an introduction by John Lasseter, and then the final pages and index listing what's on what page and artist credits. There are no pages talking about the storyboard process, how hard something was to explain with storyboards or to put in animated form, nothing of that sort. What you get are huge pictures of the storyboards with their respective notes, if any, and you can take in the artwork in all its glory. Even the we'll-probably-never-see-it-on-dvd Song of the South is featured in here! B003S9GOWS|5.0|First of all, prospective buyers should be aware that this essential Rubinstein set is still available in its earlier incarnation at just about the same bargain-basement price: [[ASIN:B000026OW3 The Chopin Collection]]. By taking better advantage of the space available, this new set has ten discs vs eleven in the earlier compilation, however, the contents are identical. The older set, which is made up of bulky multi-disc jewel cases, includes an excellent set of liner notes with essays on both Rubinstein and Chopin, whereas this new space-saving box has no notes. Finally, both sets are made from digital remasters by Rubinstein's long time producer, Max Wilcox.     Since there are numerous amazon reviews appended to the earlier set, I'll keep my remarks to a minimum here. That Rubinstein was still at the top of his form at the time the earliest of the RCA stereo sessions were recorded is attested to by the tremendous Ballades and Scherzos from 1959. Rubinstein plays these pieces with absolute authority, from both a technical and musical perspective. These were reference recordings for many years, and with good reason. The same is true of the piano concertos. The best way to describe them is that they are very sturdy but elegant, completely fresh and inspired, splashed with poetic touches and also have plenty of muscle in the virtuoso passages. The Polonaises, which were recorded in 1964, are also quite muscular, but never overplayed - that Rubinstein was 77 at the time seems a footnote. And so on.     Not everything is ideal here: for example, I've never been all that fond of Rubinstein's studio recordings of the two sonatas (a recital performance of the B-flat minor from 1961 shows the pianist in a completely different light: [[ASIN:B000059M5B Rubenstein Performs in Concert]]); and the 1946 preludes, the only set he recorded, are not among the best that I've heard. But these are minor exceptions compared to the treasures in this collection. Just listen to the Andante Spianato and Grand Polonaise from 1964, a riveting combination of virtuosity, elegance and gorgeous tone. That's what Rubinstein was all about.    There are many fine Chopin recordings in the catalog, however, so highly regarded were most of these Rubinstein recordings that for decades they were the standard by which all others were measured. Have they been superceded? Maybe, but, like virtually all of the music in this extraordinary collection, they have a strong advantage in that they reflect what must have been thousands of performances by the great Chopin pianist going back fifty or more years.    Five stars.

0811879542|5.0|On the opening page of PRESS HERE, the reader sees a simple yellow dot on a clean white background and the simple words that invite the reader to \\"Press here and turn the page.\\"  I challenge anyone not to be tempted to \\"press here\\". It should be noted that the book is designed in a way that also allows for heavy use.  With a book that encourages interactions, it is critical that it can stand up to lots of handling. B003WUYOQ6|5.0|It's pretty hard to argue with the science. Unless one wants to start proclaiming that Taubes is out and out lying to us in order to reach some sort of evil aim, this latest book is a definitive read on, well, why we get fat and what to do about it. I have been a convinced reader of Taubes ever since reading 'Good Calories, Bad Calories' a year or two ago. Many people who believe in a controlled-carb way of eating, follow an Atkins or Atkins-variation type way of eating. Dr. Atkins' New Diet Revolution (DANDR) is considered among the pantheon of 'diet' books. In it, Dr. Atkins laid out his 'way' and said, basically, \\"This is what works and my experience and research supports this, trust me!\\". Taubes, in Good Calories, Bad Calories (GCBC), basically said, \\"Here's everything ever done on the science, research, and politics of nutrition.\\" It was a pretty dense read but enlightening to those who made the effort to follow and understand. As a sort of scientific investigative journalist, Taubes was exhaustive in his research and following the trail. His interpretation of the results is hard to argue with. However, as earlier stated, GCBC is a pretty intense read. 'Why We Get Fat: And What to do About It' really bridges the HOW of DANDR with the WHY of GCBC in an easy and enticing read. WWGF tells you why and says, \\"Here's what the science tells us and here's what it means to you\\". I never believed that I would recommend a 'diet' book as being a page-turner I couldn't put down 'til I found out how it all ended but I found myself spell-bound and read it cover to cover in two sittings. I'll definitely be reading and referring to it again and again in the future. Even those already well-versed in the way of controlled-carb eating will find this an informative read. We've known that 'X' is the way things work but sometimes not really easily understood the why of it. Taubes not only reinforces that 'X' does indeed work but also says, 'Y' is the reason -- all in a clear, concise essay.<br /><br />Note for the Kindle readers: This entire book is well laid out and formatted for the Kindle version. If all Kindle books were this well formatted, I might never buy another hard-copy book ever again.essay. Schumacher's eye for the various landscapes/cityscapes of L.A. is piercing and really gives us a sense that it's a gigantic devil's playground, and the overall growing sense of foreboding and doom is palpable.<br /><br />But all of that would mean nothing if the central performance wasn't up to snuff, and fortunately it is. D-Fens is a great example of this kind of personality, one that I think a lot of us have buried deep inside us, though most of us are able to keep it under control.<br /><br />Anyway I'd like to write more about this one sometime, but I think I'll stop at this. The DVD is as others have mentioned pretty bare-bones; this would profit from a good commentary track I think, especially if Schumacher and Douglas could be persuaded to do it. 0061474355|4.0|There are good reasons why Garth Nix's fantasy about a girl who battles evil beings from the realm of Death as she searches for her barely living father should have gained a faithful following. Though Nix uses elements common to many fantasies, he succeeds by creating a unique yet convincing world, fashioning a main character we can hold to our hearts, and secondary characters that inject just enough humor and romance to keep things from getting too dreary. That's not to say this is a literary masterpiece, but it's damn good entertainment. Let's take a look at what works, and what its limitations are.<br /><br />WHAT WORKS<br /><br />1. Nix's world: It's fascinatingly poised, not only between life and death, but between the past and the present. A rustic army, evocative of World War I, guards a border between the Old Kingdom and a country named Ancelstierre; like most fun fantasy novels there's a cool map showing the layout. The army has guns and motor vehicles, but often have to resort to using swords, arrows, etc. to fight off the magically endowed beings from Death. Magic comes from something called the Charter, a kind of magical religion Nix never completely explains, and Sabriel is born into a line of Charter sorcerers called the Abhorsen, whose task it is to fight dark magic with light magic and send dead things back to Death permanently.<br /><br />In Nix's cosmology, one doesn't cross a river to enter Death: Death IS the river, and as it carries your soul along you pass through a number of gates; pass through the Seventh Gate and you're an absolute goner. As a child Sabriel spent time with her father in Death, which explains their pale skin. For reasons having to do with Charter Magic, it's easier to traverse Death in the Old Kingdom, both for the Abhorsen and the \\"Free Magic\\" zombie-like beings who devour living humans to keep themselves alive.<br /><br />The world of The Old Kingdom/Ancelstierre is tightly focused. There are vague references to the civilized lands further south, away from the Old Kingdom, where presumably the very idea of Magic is pooh-poohed. What lives north of the Old Kingdom is never touched on, nor what lies across the seas to the east and west. Nix's world draws its defining presence by the Border, which like many borders is rife with tension. Other fantasies--I'm thinking Neil Gaiman's Stardust--feature a border between two worlds. But Nix's is unique in that one side of the border has a foot in two worlds, making for a spooky uncertainty once one crosses that border.<br /><br />2. Nix's protagonist: Sabriel is sincere and likeable, passionate and determined, and unfazed by the horrors thrown at her. To work magic over things from Death she uses a set of named bells that hang on a bandolier draped over her shoulder. This invention serves not only to draw us into this world, they become part of Sabriel's character. They give us an immediate visual and auditory impression of her.<br /><br />2. Secondary characters: Another major character is a cat named Mogget, who helps Sabriel despite the fact that he is really a devil ensorcelled by her father--a devil who, once unleashed, wants nothing more than to destroy Sabriel. This adds interesting tension, and partly explains Mogget's contradictory behavior. (On the other hand, he IS a cat...)<br /><br />The third major character is a prince named Touchstone, whom Sabriel rescues from a 200-year spell, spent bound up as a ship's prow: an interesting inversion of the Sleeping Beauty trope. Unfortunately I didn't find Touchstone's character to be as interesting. Until he comes through in the end and shows his Magic stuff, he's kind of a clueless klutz. I actually found a more interesting male character to be Colonel Horyse (does Nix have a thing about horses?), a kind-hearted army officer whose job is to defend the Quotidian World with weapons that are pretty useless once a Big Nasty crosses the border in a wave of rolling fog (much like a Death Metal concert, come to think of it).<br /><br />3. Nix's style: His third-person narrative is sympathetic, sometimes dryly humorous and sometimes highly evocative, but never over-the-top. It's an easy read; his prose flows smoothly, and I didn't find my mind wandering; nor did his lack of explaining things like Charter Magic get in the way. His unnamed narrator feels comfortable in his world, and tells the story without making an issue of being a storyteller. For me, the one character in the story who feels closest to the narrator to is Colonel Horyse.<br /><br />LIMITATIONS<br /><br />This is an uncomplicated story: no perspective jumping, different voices, extended flashbacks, or other narrative tricks. His main character, though strong and true, displays few nuanced inner conflicts or personality traits. And in the popular tradition that demands Good and Evil, there's no doubting the evil nature of the Mordicant or Kerrigor, the two great Death antagonists Sabriel has to defeat. The only ambiguous character in the tale is Mogget, who lacks the sophisticated complexity of, say, a Severus Snape.<br /><br />More importantly, Nix draws on material that has a well-worn, comic book quality: the heroine suffering at the disgusting hands of an Evil Lord who desires her; the demons with fires in their mouths, the cowering townfolk beset by evil things, magic swords, etc. For younger fans who crave this sort of thing, such tropes are positive, not negative, but they do relegate Sabriel to fantasy genre status. While I have yet to read the other volumes in the series, I wouldn't expect them to explore ambiguous heroes or turn Good vs. Evil on its head.<br /><br />CONCLUSION<br /><br />If you like fantasy, with well-drawn worlds and interesting characters, Sabriel is a good read. And if you like Death and stuff like that, it's a great read. If you don't like fantasy, hey, there's always Pride and Prejudice...right?rd-person narrative is sympathetic, sometimes dryly humorous and sometimes highly evocative, but never over-the-top. It's an easy read; his prose flows smoothly, and I didn't find my mind wandering; nor did his lack of explaining things like Charter Magic get in the way. His unnamed narrator feels comfortable in his world, and tells the story without making an issue of being a storyteller. For me, the one character in the story who feels closest to the narrator to is Colonel Horyse. His main character, though strong and true, displays few nuanced inner conflicts or personality traits. And in the popular tradition that demands Good and Evil, there's no doubting the evil nature of the Mordicant or Kerrigor, the two great Death antagonists Sabriel has to defeat. The only ambiguous character in the tale is Mogget, who lacks the sophisticated complexity of, say, a Severus Snape.    More importantly, Nix draws on material that has a well-worn, comic book quality: the heroine suffering at the disgusting hands of an Evil Lord who desires her; the demons with fires in their mouths, the cowering townfolk beset by evil things, magic swords, etc. For younger fans who crave this sort of thing, such tropes are positive, not negative, but they do relegate Sabriel to fantasy genre status. While I have yet to read the other volumes in the series, I wouldn't expect them to explore ambiguous heroes or turn Good vs. Evil on its head.     CONCLUSION    If you like fantasy, with well-drawn worlds and interesting characters, Sabriel is a good read. And if you like Death and stuff like that, it's a great read. If you don't like fantasy, hey, there's always Pride and Prejudice...right?

B0034G4OXQ|2.0|I have liked almost every film Clint Eastwood has directed, but with this story, he made lots of choices that just did not work for me. First, a word about the successful parts: I sympathized with the main character played by Matt Damon, a reluctant psychic who would like to \\"retire\\" from the practice of giving readings but who keeps encountering people who need the hope that such contacts bring. I used to give readings myself. I was trained in my mid-20's in a small church of the American Spiritualist Association, in Albuquerque. I did mediumistic readings frequently for two years, never for money, and only part-time. Many people claimed that what came through me, but not supposedly from me, was helpful. However, I grew uneasy with the practice over time, and gave fewer and fewer until doing my final one in 1984, eleven years after first learning how. I met people who only wanted their pre-determined beliefs confirmed, and people who wanted \\"guidance\\" way too frequently, and people who \\"channel-shopped\\" until they got \\"spiritual permission\\" to do what they wished to do anyway. So Matt Damon's dilemma in this film resonated with me.    Ah, but Mr. Eastwood, you mucked up this tale of moral conflict mightily. The first two-thirds of your movie is so terribly slow, and sad, and much of it is in French, and I almost gave up on your film. Not everyone in grief is so paralyzed that he or she talks in whispers without need, or makes every physical motion take twice as long as it should. There was no real cause to make one character French and spend so much time in the details of her news anchor job. No real cause, either, to make another character British, and spend so much time showing his mom's drug abuse. All these atmospherics and opportunities to show the skylines of Paris and London do not add enough to your story line to justify the time you took doing them. I found neither the French woman nor the English boy sympathetic enough to make me root for them overmuch. The last 40 minutes were pretty good, but a film with such a title and theme should have expended more screen time showing and explaining the hereafter and less on the unpleasant details of the earthly lives of your characters.

With Firefly, I saw sci-fi in a way I had never seen it before. I had of course heard of the term \\"space western\\", and have seen elements of that concept in shows like Star Trek and in movies like Star Wars and Avatar. However, until now I had never seen the \\"space western\\" theme realized in such a literal way. The juxtaposition between spaceships and horses, and space stations and frontier towns, is a delight to watch. The storylines are sometimes straight out of westerns, such as the train heist in \\"The Train Job\\". Josh Whedon, however, flawlessly interweaves the two seemingly at-odds thematic elements, and the result is 14 phenomenal episodes (and a great followup theatrical release, \\"Serenity\\"). There is an equal mix of drama, humor and action in this series, and just when you think you know what direction an episode is going to take, Whedon takes it in a totally different direction. Rarely is television so well-scripted, especially these days.<br /><br />Interesting concept aside, no television show is worth much if the characters are not believable or interesting. Firefly has great character development. All the main characters are (through both scripting and acting) fully fleshed-out human beings who we grow to deeply care about as we progress through the series. You can tell that the actors really cared about their roles, because their performances effortlessly exude the characters as they were conceived by Whedon. Through these portrayals come the series' great strength - a tale of desperate people of disparate backgrounds who find themselves linked in a particular place and time. We see the love and anger, the humor and sadness, and we identify with them. We want to be with them on Serenity, living the lives that they lead.<br /><br />All in all, I think that the Fox network executive who made the decision to dump this series should be hog-tied and deposited on the deck of a ship full of starving, amorous Reavers. Television is almost never this good, and if Firefly had been allowed to run its originally-planned 7 full seasons, I believe it would be referred to by the general TV-viewing public as one of the all-time great series.<br /><br />Even if you're not a fan of sci-fi, get this anyway. This series, first and foremost, is about people, not spaceships.the result is 14 phenomenal episodes (and a great followup theatrical release, \\"Serenity\\"). There is an equal mix of drama, humor and action in this series, and just when you think you know what direction an episode is going to take, Whedon takes it in a totally different direction. In short, Karr wants to promote an alternative way of being, but she seems to have internalized damaging and misogynist values that remain unexamined. B003CN5SAY|1.0|This is a review for the entire show and not just Season 6.    Seasons 1 and 2 of Nip/Tuck were brilliant. And I mean it. I think the writing was one of the best in television history: intelligent, engaging, thought provoking, edgy and just breathtaking. It set the standard for TV dramas and writing in all subsequent years if you ask me. The story lines were believable yet provocative and the topics picked were right at the heart of the matter. Because the characters were written with so much depth, layer and nuances, their struggles were believable and the audience could identify and often sympathize. For the first two years I really believed this to have been the best TV drama I had ever seen.     Unfortunately, it all went downhill starting Season 3. I dont know if they changed writers or just got blinded by their success and didnt try as hard, but Nip/Tuck made a downhill run starting in Season 3 until the abysmal failure which was the last season: increasingly improbable, far fetched situations that take the notion of suspending disbelief to new heights, bad to no character development (at least not one that is believable), unimaginative writing that is all over the place, everyone acting supremely out of character...I could go on with more examples. There just wasn't much depth in the characters later on and in fact at some point they became a parody of themselves it seems. The writers took the \\"sudden twists\\" in plot development that were gripping and enticing in the first two seasons and sprinkled them excessively in every episode to the point where it became almost ridiculous. While in the beginning they used those sparingly to get the desired effect, it was just overdone in later seasons - ultimately causing the show to lose exactly what it tried to accomplish.     As mentioned above, people behaving out of character was another prominent feature of the later seasons:     Yes, Christian Troy is a womanizer and playboy who loves luxury, hot girls and shinycars, but he is also a wounded soul, a caring man who deep down has demons to fight while at the same time trying to hold on to certain principles he would never relinquish. In later seasons, they just turned him into a vile, ruthless, unsympathetic, one dimensional animal who is lacking a moral center. While he had some endearing qualities in the beginning, he had none in the end. He was a weak, mean coward who lost his edge and humanity.     Sean, who was always the thoughtful, loyal, dedicated and responsible person and especially father, was turned into a big time jerk and sex maniac in the later seasons - a man who had no interest in his family and his children and who slept around and used people.     The same is true with most of the characters really. Julia, (Joely Richardson), was a driving force in this series. Her struggles of wanting to go to med school and being more than just a house wife, as well as her subdued, unspoken love and attraction for Christian were an important relationship and plot element. Gradually, she made fewer and fewer appearances, however, became a lesbian at some point (yeah right) - stating she had always been one - and then she sort of disappeared for extended periods of time without explanation or closure. Her passion for medical school and her own life were never even touched upon again and she herself disappeared into obscurity. That was sad, because Julia's presence and the tensions that had developed between her, Christian and Sean were at the heart of the show and its drama. They spent two years building all that up just to drop the subject altogether and never revisit it again.     Speaking of unresolved: Gina just completely disappeared at some point without explanation and then came back just to die in the most absurd, ridiculous way you can imagine: she fell during sex out of the balcony. I really thought they were kidding and this was just someone's day dream or imagination but lo and behold, that was it: she really fell off the balcony during sex. Not only was that ridiculous but also sad in a way and just insulting to what the writers had done with the character in the first place. I always found Gina very fascinating and tragic; the self destruction, low self esteem, the fact that she was Wilbur's mother and her battle with HIV. Her character was filled with potential and they just let her fizzle out and then die in this embarrassing, trashy manner. Totally insulting. I wonder what Christian will tell Wilbur when he asks about his mother one day.     There is also Kimber, another deeply tragic character who ended up being very underutilized given the potential the writers had with her. They did an amazing job exposing her self destructive tendencies and low self esteem, but then just like that, they dropped her and she too became a two-dimensional paper doll and a clich√©. Christian marries her on and off over the seasons, but nothing in their dynamic with each other changes. She doesnt develop as a character for either the better or the worst. I saw a glimpse of hope when she got that abortion and it turned out she could never have kids again - gosh that entire plot segment was filled with potential for her character and the implications of that for Christian - but instead of keeping going on that strong note, they just dropped it and let it fade away. The same thing happened when she died. Did she commit suicide? Is she dead? Is she alive? What exactly happened? We never find out. Her death was not remarkable to anyone - which it should have and could have been had the writers not been so busy making up far fetched bogus stories.     There is also that entire plot of Christian marrying Liz. Speak of behaving out of character: Liz always held herself and others to the highest standards. She had a disdain for Chrstian's superficiality and how he used and treated women and she was fully aware of it too. She would *never* have stooped to his level and married him. That was justnot her. But they put it in the script and made her whine after Christian and it was ridiculous.     If these were changes in plot and character the writers intended, they should have gone about it in a different way (even though there is no excuse for some of the outlandish plot twists they introduced). People dont just change overnight and fiction or not, you still have to adhere to certain rules such as character consistency within a plot. Sean was portrayed from the beginning on as a hard working, ethical, dedicated father. It is completely out of character for him to suddenly turn into a vile jerk who uses women and doesnt care about his kids. Liz would never have fallen for Christian's sweet talk and married him and then pine away after him, that is ridiculous - not to mention that she is a lesbian.     Of course it is possible for people to change but there has to be evidence for it in the story and plot and not simply because the script says so. The later seasons are drizzled with even more such inconsistencies, which is a sign of bad writing more than one of just things having changed due to the circumstances the characters find themselves . Acting out of character is not character development.     Finally, changing the setting from Miami to L.A. aided in ruining the show completely. The L.A. setting made them be nothing but a bunch of cheap, trashy, run of the mill Beverly Hills plastic surgeons trying to find a way to sell out to the media by humiliating themselves - sort of like the Kardassians. There is some kind of a mystery, sexiness and just boldness to Miami which L.A.'s theme-park commercialism lacks. In the show, L.A. was portrayed as a den for greedy TV producers and dumb people with non-existent moral codes willing to do anything for a dollar, a d-- ck and a dream. McNamara and Troy just became pawns in that scheme and the stories shifted from them to Los Angeles, the dirt of that town and the despicable people roaming it (to the point of stereotyping the city). That was a bad move which, in my opinion, pretty much sealed Nip/Tuck's ruin and reduced its quality to just any other forgettable TV show you hope to end.     The bottom line is that after the success of the first two seasons, the show's producers and writers put the characters into increasingly more improbable situations to the point of turning them into clich√©s of themselves and the show as such; coupled with the issues mentioned above - such as character inconsistency and poor, chaotic plot development - the result has been that over time a serious disconnect has been created and once you have too many of those disconnects, you lose credibility. And I think that is the main breaking point in the end: the show lost a lot of credibility, unintentionally became a farce of itself and with that it lost the edge and brilliance that made it so amazing and intense in the first place.

0307595862|5.0|It's hard to review a mystery or detective novel without giving too much away. If it's a good one, you wanna grasp by the shoulder everyone you know and say, \\"This was SO good! Let me tell you....\\"  If the friend is also a book reader chances are they will say, \\"Stop! I haven't read that one yet!\\" and you're forced to keep mum while you relive the plot as it swirls inside your noggin.<br /><br />At first I didn't like this book. About a month ago I finished my first Scandinavian thriller/mystery, Faceless Killers by Henning Mankell. When I began Jo Nesb√∏'s Snowman too many things stood out to me as rehash of the first Kurt Wallander. Both make ample use of the down-on-his-luck police inspector who's harsh personal life is just as prone to tragedy as his police work. Both authors also use the icy climates of their region to great effect, to the point that the weather is as much as a character as any person. Until the halfway point at least I let my reading of a previous thriller set in this locale hinder and even cloud my enjoyment of Nesb√∏'s novel. After I dropped the comparison and began to enjoy the book for what it is, I found that while the novels are similar in terms of 'feel', each author truly has his own unique spin on the world around him.<br /><br />With Stieg Larsson's Millennium-trilogy (which I'm waiting to read until I have a few more examples under my belt with which to contrast/compare) the publishing world has gone nutso for all things Scandinavian. For the last ten years or more I've enjoyed the music scenes of Norway and Sweden and with the easy availability of these novels I hope to read as many as possible so that I might get a verbal tour of each country's major towns and culture. A well rounded experience to go along with the music.<br /><br />The Snowman is Nesb√∏'s seventh novel in the Harry Hole series and the first that I have read. The first two have not yet made their way to English translations. I was hesitant to begin so late in the series but when I saw this book in the Vine newsletter last month I couldn't resist. In Snowman Inspector Harry Hole is on the trail of a supposed serial killer who may or may not have ties to a series of killings twelve years previous. I don't have any previous knowledge of the series with which to compare, but Nesb√∏ did a good job of filling in a reader new to the series by helpful character tags and characters reminiscing about previous cases and other characters now gone. In several of these reminiscences I was forced (obligingly) to add previous novels to my To Be Read pile because I thought, Now that sounds like a good story!<br /><br />Going back to my introduction, it's hard to comment on all the twists and turns in this novel without feeling like you may ruin the experience for another reader. In short, I will say that the twists and turns almost wore me out (my brain felt like a corkscrew!) but there was not a time that I didn't find myself wanting to read the novel. For the last year or so I've been reading two novels at once, sometimes because it helps me through a boring novel that I may put down otherwise but when read in conjunction made the boring novel seem like a nice bit of fluff between a more \\"heady\\" read. With Snowman, I read this book and ONLY this book until I was finished. Even early on when I was less than certain about how much I would enjoy this compared to my previous experience with Kurt Wallander (who I loved and can't wait to read more about), I still took every opportunity to snatch up the book and read a chapter. I think this is the kind of book that the phrase \\"compulsively readable\\" was invented to describe.<br /><br />This mystery, if you don't much care for it at the onset, will grow on you. I can only imagine that it will fester if you enjoyed it from the start. There is a strong sense of foreboding throughout and the use of \\"mold\\" as a metaphor is both humorous and all too fitting. This was my first read as part of Amazon Vine and I can't think of a better book with which to feel inaugurated into the program.with which to feel inaugurated into the program.

I appreciate what he did, but I would have loved this song had he kept a straight blues-y Bill Withers sound he introduces the song with. I know James Blake's music is in part defined by auto-tuning, but he really could have staved off it here.<br /><br />Much of this album felt like an idea that didn't quite manifest itself. Many of the songs begin with an interesting mix of concrete melodies with electronica, but degrade to a default and ungenuine auto-tune-clicks-and-whistles midi-mashup. Some songs, like \\"Lindisfarne I\\" and \\"Lindisfarne II\\", could have had a bit more melodic structure from the beginning to give them more purpose and direction. Other songs made me think that he just ran out of steam. I would have really liked it if he had expanded more on \\"Why Don't You Call Me?\\".<br /><br />After listening to this album, I immediately pictured an anxious kid in his room recording this hoping for acknowledgement for being hip and edgy. I found myself yelling at my speakers: \\"I get it! You don't have to convince me that you are cool anymore.\\" In his self-titled album, James Blake exhibits flashes of his deep musicality that goes beyond the world of electronica and some interesting musical concepts, however, I feel like he often gets stuck on this and just runs his songs into the ground.<br /><br />More than anything, I agree with Lone Wolf: Please stop labeling this \\"like Bon Iver\\". I know James Blake claims that they are an influence, but the genres they exist in aren't even really compatible. Moreover, Bon Iver imbues their music with a raw soulfulness and passion that I'm not even sure electronica music can convey. They just don't compare.midi-mashup. B003YFIUW8|5.0|(very slight but not very spoily spoilers ahead)    For everyone reading the one-star reviews and wondering if this is a book they want to purchase, please give it a chance.  It's really amazing, and the narration is captivating.  I won't drone on in defense of this novel.  I would LIKE to.  But I'll say this: Have some imagination.  Get down on your hands and knees.  Forget about what you know about the world.  See things not from your 43-year-old businessman suit-and-tie world, but from that of a five-year-old's point of view.  When you hear Jack tell you about Room and Ma, pretend you don't know ANYTHING.  Stop being an adult, stop having opinions, stop thinking about your own Room, which is sure to be bigger and wider than Jack's.  Just listen.  Don't assume anything.  Don't read this and feel smart knowing that you know more about the world than Jack and therefore he's not a believable character.  If all you had was what Jack had, would you be \\"normal\\"?  If you went through the same dramatic situations, wouldn't your personality be inconsistent?  To me, it's all perfectly fitting for a five-year-old, and even more understandable given the abrupt changes to his reality.      Consider his singular situation before you come to the conclusion that he shouldn't talk as he does, or act as he does.  Don't apply YOUR childhood to Jack's.  If you do, you will ruin this remarkable story.

Spencer, by far my favorite, who is intelligent and snarky and has to deal with an increasingly hellish home life. B004FN7ZOC|5.0|I have been a fan of Amon Amarth since their \\"Versus the World\\" album. I was drawn to their melodic death sound and Viking-influenced lyrics since the song \\"Death in Fire\\". I am not highly knowledgeable about death and black metal, nor do I pretend to be. I can only relate this Amon Amarth to my own listening experience. There sound is based on a cookie monster-type death vocal that reminds me of the best lyrical moments of Six Feet Under, Arch Enemy, Mortification, and Fear Factory. However I think Amon Amarth's vocalist has a higher quality voice with more sustain and a greater range than most death vocalists, with the exception of Arch Enemy's Angela Gossow. Amon Amarth uses a guitar duo that is detuned but not excessively detuned. They don't play many solos, but they play a lot of guitar melodies and harmonies that have the effect of making the pitchless vocals sound more melodic. The drums are driven by double-bass, but they are not headache inducing like a lot of similar bands are. Their lyrics are about Norse mythology, Vikings, riding/sailing off to war, fate, valiant death, and meeting one's ancestors.<br /><br />Amon Amarth are a band that is known for doing the same thing over and over, but that is not a bad thing. They are almost a Viking melodo-death answer to AC/DC, a band that does the same thing for an entire career and makes it somehow comforting, familiar, and totally awesome rather than ever getting old. \\"Surtur Rising\\" is more of the same in their usual vein, but somehow they do it better. Their songwriting is more focused, guitar melodies better written, lyrics more interesting, performance is higher quality, and production value is way higher than anything they have done since Versus the World. There are probably no songs here quite as catchy as \\"Death in Fire\\" and \\"Across the Rainbow Bridge,\\" but some of these songs come close. This album is slightly better than \\"With Oden on Our Side\\" and much better than \\"Fate of Norms\\". If you like metal and are considering Amon Amarth, you can't go wrong with this album. If you are already a fan of the band, this is one of their best.<br /><br />Plus there is a long bonus live DVD consisting of 33 tracks from a complete concert  in 2008. The DVD has very high audio and video quality, and I am totally blown away by the band's stamina and ability to play such a long set.nsidering Amon Amarth, you can't go wrong with this album. Japanese began assigning handling a body to strangers considered unclean stigmatized outcasts engaged in a disreputable occupation, nokanshi. Daigo is rejected by his childhood friend, son of the bath house owner, because of his profession as nokanshi.<br /><br />Daigo, who has not been forthcoming about the nature of his work with his wife, feels unclean after handling bodies and decamps to his neighborhood bathhouse. As a child he was sheltered by the bathhouse owner as he mourned his father's abandonment. Visiting the bathhouse becomes a marital ritual. Daigo has not revealed his occupation to his wife. Both are befriended by the attentive hard working bath house owner, gentle carer who cuts wood, stokes the fire, carries water. Daigo creates a surrogate extended family with &#34;the boss&#34; mentor and the warm benevolence of the nurturing bathhouse owner.<br /><br />The March 11, 2011 earthquake, tsunami and after shocks present Japanese with the challenge of handling bodies with respectful dignity while expelling rubble. Numbers of bodies present with disfigurement, overwhelming the capacity to offer composed honorable farewell, or most disconcerting, not finding the bodies at all.<br /><br />Writer Kundo Takita encapsulates the diverse responses to death in a melange of families encountered by apprentice nokanshi Daigo as he learns from Sasaki how to encoffin a beloved grandfather, a baseball-loving child, a revered and loved grandmother disfigured by long illness, an adolescent cross-dresser. Daigo's nokanshi rituals restore pain ravaged families to harmony with calm meditative tradition; he finds meaning through gentle ritual and cello meditation.<br /><br />Okurobito the Japanese film name won multiple awards in Asia and Oscar for Best Foreign Film in 2009.
